Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúI have made Indra glorified by these two, heaven and earth, and this prayer of Vi≈õvƒÅmitra protects the race of Bharata.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Made Indra glorified: indram atu·π£·π≠avam– the verb is the third preterite of the casual, I have caused to be praised; it may mean: I praise Indra, abiding between heaven and earth, i.e. in the firmament

Jamison Brereton

I who have praised both these two world-halves here and Indra‚Äî Vi≈õvƒÅmitra‚Äôs sacred formulation here guards the Bharata people.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The first hemistich, couched in the 1st ps., consists only of a rel. clause, which breaks off. The ‚ÄúI‚Äù is clearly Vi≈õvƒÅmitra, whose name opens the hemistich and whose protective br√°hman- is mentioned there – making it very likely that pƒÅdas ab constitute this br√°hman-, though it‚Äôs not quite clear what is protective about this truncated utterance.

The plupf. √°tu·π£·π≠avam should not exist, at least in my opinion, since the two forms of the indicative pf. tu·π£·π≠uv√∫·∏• (VIII.6.12, 18) also appear to have preterital value. However, the contexts in VIII.6 do not guarantee that value – it is possible that they are presential ‚Äúthey praise‚Äù (see comm. ad VIII.6.12). The existence of a pf.

subj. tu·π£·π≠√°vat at VIII.98.16 also suggests that the indicative pf. is, or originally was, presential. The only other pf. forms in the RV, the act. part. tu·π£·π≠uv√°·πÉs- (3x) and mid.

part. tu·π£·π≠uvƒÅn√°- (1x) are generally tr. as preterital, but again context does not dictate this rendering.

Praises to Indra have I sung, sustainer of this earth and heaven. This prayer of Visvamitra keeps secure the race of Bharatas.

(‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§á‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Ø‡•á (‡§â‡§≠‡•á) ‡§¶‡•ã‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç (‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§ß‡§® ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° (‡§á‡§¶‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® (‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§â‡§∏ (‡§ú‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§ø) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏ (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§π‡§Æ (‡§Ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∏ (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ! ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∞‡§ö‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø ‡§á‡§Æ‡•á ‡§â‡§≠‡•á ‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§π‡§Æ‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§§ ‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡§É) (‡§á‡§Æ‡•á) (‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å (‡§â‡§≠‡•á) (‡§Ö‡§π‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡•á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§É (‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§ø) (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ (‡§á‡§¶‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ã‡§Ω‡§™‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ú‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•á‡§®‡•á‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§§‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§§ ‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§¨ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã ! ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§∏‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§ß‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á ‡§â‡§∏ (‡§á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§π‡•Ä (‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•á) ‡§ß‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§§) ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á, ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ! ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§∏‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•à ‡§á‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£ ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§§ ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§É (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§§) ‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§ß‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡•á (‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•á) ‡§ß‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•á (‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§á‡§§‡•ç) ‡§è‡§µ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§∏‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

English translation:

‚ÄúWhat do the cattle for you among the Kƒ´ka·π≠as; they yield no milk to mix with the Soma, they need not the vessel (for the libation); bring them to us; (bring also) the wealth of the son of the usurer, and give us Maghavan, (the possessions) of the low branches (of the community).‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The Kƒ´ka·π≠as: (Nirukta 6.32) are people who do not perform worship, who are infidels, nƒÅstikas; in countrie sinhabited by anƒÅryas (kƒ´ka·π≠ƒÅ nƒÅma de≈õonƒÅryanivƒÅsa·∏•); na tapanti gharmƒÅ·πái: harmyam = a house;

GharmƒÅ·πái = a vessel termed mahƒÅvƒ´ra used at the rite called prag·πõhya: prag·πõh yƒÅkhyƒÅ karmopa yuktam mahƒÅvƒ´rapƒÅtram, which the cattle do not warm by yielding their milk to it;

Usurer: ƒÅ bhara pramagandasya vedas: maganda = kusidin, or usurer, one who says to himself, the money that goes from me will come back doubled, and pra = a patronymic; low branches of the community: naicƒÅ≈õƒÅkham, that which belongs to a low (nƒ´ca) branch, or class (≈õƒÅkha); the posterity born of ≈ö≈´dras and the like

Jamison Brereton

What do the cows do for you among the Kƒ´ka·π≠as? They do not milk out the milk mixture; they do not heat the gharma[=hot]-drink.
Bring here to us the possessions of Pramaganda. Make the descendant of Nƒ´cƒÅ≈õƒÅkha subject to us, bounteous one.

Griffith

Among the Kikatas what do thy cattle? They pour no milky draught, they heat no caldron.
Bring thou to us the wealth of Pramaganda;give up to us, O Maghavan, the low-born.

Geldner

Was n√ºtzen dir die K√ºhe bei den Kikata¬¥s? Sie melken keine Mischmilch f√ºr den Soma, sie machen keinen Gharmatrank hei√ü. Bring uns die Habe des Pramaganda her, unterwirf uns den Naicasakha, du Gabenreicher!

Grassmann

Was n√ºtzen dir die K√ºhe bei den Kƒ´katern, nicht lassen sie Mischtrank str√∂men, noch Br√ºhe sieden; bringe uns her die Habe des geizigen Wucherers, das niedrige Gesindel, o m√§chtiger, √ºberliefere uns.

(‡§§‡•á) ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•á (‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∏‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡§É) ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§®) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§Ü‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§ß ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡•á) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§®) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§§‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§§‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á (‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§ï‡•É‡§£‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡§ó‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡•Ä‡§® ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ù‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•á (‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§É) ‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ü) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á (‡§≠‡§∞) ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§π‡•á (‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§ß‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®‡•à‡§ö‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§ñ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä (‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø) ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ ‡§ú‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä, ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•Ä ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¨‡§≤ ‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§á‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§§‡•ç‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç ! ‡§§‡•á ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•ã ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ω‡§Ω‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§§‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§£‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§Ü ‡§≠‡§∞‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡•ã ‡§®‡•à‡§ö‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§§‡•á) ‡§§‡§µ (‡§ï‡•É‡§£‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) (‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§∑‡•Å (‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡§É) ‡§ß‡•á‡§®‡•Ç‡§É (‡§®) (‡§Ü‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡•á) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§®) (‡§§‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) (‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ò‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§π‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡•¶‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§Ç‡•¶‡•ß‡•§ ‡•Ø‡•§ (‡§Ü) ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§≠‡§∞) ‡§ß‡§∞ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡•Ä‡§®‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§É) ‡§ß‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§®‡•à‡§ö‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§ñ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ö‡§æ ‡§∂‡§æ‡§ñ‡§æ ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§™‡•Ç‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§ß‡§®‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø) ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•ã ‡§® ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö, ‡§§‡§•‡•à‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¨‡§≤‡§æ ‡§® ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§•‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á. ‡§ú‡§∂‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§Ç‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§§ ‡§ó‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Ä‡§ö‡•Ä, ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§§ ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ‡§¢ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§ ‡§®‡§æ‡§π‡•Ä, ‡§§‡§∏‡•á‡§ö ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§® ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§£‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¨‡§≥ ‡§π‡•ã‡§ä ‡§®‡§Ø‡•á‡§§ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§†‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§∏‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä‡§≤ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡•á. ‡•• ‡•ß‡•™ ‡••

Jamison Brereton Notes

As indicated in the published introduction, the subject of these two vss. is the mysterious feminine sasarparƒ´ÃÅ-, which has been interpr. as differently as ‚ÄúKriegstrompete‚Äù (BR, fld. by Grassmann), ‚ÄúSangesgeweise‚Äù of the Vi≈õvƒÅmitras (Geldner), and VƒÅc (Anukrama·πáƒ´, SƒÅya·πáa.). The interpr. of these vss. has been further complicated by the later tradition that sees them as concerning the supposed rivalry between Vi≈õvƒÅmitra and Vasi·π£·π≠ha, for which I see no evidence at all in the RV.

Although I do not think all the puzzles are ultimately solvable, some clues can get us some distance. First, sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- is a v·πõkƒ´-type fem., and as Debrunner points out (AiG II.2.369), the major use of this inflectional type is for female beings (human and animal). This lends some credence to the opinion that the sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- is a cow of some sort (e.g., Renou ‚ÄúLa (vache) Sasarparƒ´‚Äù). That vs. 14 concerns the ritually worthless cows of the Kƒ´ka·π≠as would also support a contrast with an eminently worthy cow found among us. Second, these two vss. sound rather like a dƒÅnastuti (see jam√°dagnidatta- in 15b and yƒÅÃÅm me palastijamadagn√°yo dad√∫·∏• in 16d), and since the next part of the hymn goes off in a completely different direction, this could serve as a hymn-capping dƒÅnastuti for what precedes. Cf. I.126.2, a dƒÅnastuti hymn, where, after Kak·π£ƒ´vant is given cows, he stretches the king’s unaging fame to heaven: div√≠ ≈õr√°vo ‚Äòj√°ram ƒÅÃÅtatƒÅna, highly reminiscent of our 2nd hemistich ‚Ä¶ tatƒÅna, ≈õr√°vo dev√©·π£v am·πõÃÅtam ajury√°m.

Even if this sketch of the function of the vss. and of Sasarparƒ´ is accepted (a big if), it remains to analyze the word. I consider it a portmanteau pun. On the one hand it is a kind of anagram for the intensive of ‚àös·πõp ‚Äòcreep‚Äô, found in the RV only as the hapax adj. sarƒ´s·πõp√°- (X.162.3), which I tr. ‚Äòsquirming‚Äô. On the other hand, it is also phonologically reminiscent of sabar-d√∫gha-, -duh- ‚Äòsap-yielding‚Äô, of milk cows

– two occurrences of which are found in nearby III.55 (vss. 12, 16) qualifying Night and Dawn, one of whom bellows (mimƒÅya as here) in vs. 13. (Acdg. to Griffith, Grassmann associates Sasarparƒ´ with SabardughƒÅ, though this is not registered in the dictionary.) Just as the Sasarparƒ´ brings fame in 16, so does a sabard√∫h- in VI.48.12-13 ‚Äúmilk out immortal fame‚Äù (≈õr√°v√≤ ‚Äòm·πõtyu dh√∫k·π£ata). Another possible association is sarp√≠s- ‚Äòmelted butter‚Äô, adduced by Mayrhofer as a possible relative of sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- (EWA s.v. sarp√≠s-). My tr. ‚Äúsquirming, sappy (cow called) Sasarparƒ´‚Äù reflects my sense that all of these words have contributed to the designation sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- and these contributions are positive: sabard√∫gha- and sarp√≠s- reflect the fecundity and richness associated with juice and fat, sarƒ´s·πõpa- the uncontainable vitality of a squirming young animal.

Needless to say, this is highly speculative and does not rest on properly chaste etymological principles, but it is difficult to see what could with regard to this maddening but phonologically delectable word.

The next question to ask is why Sasarparƒ´ ‚Äúbanishes neglect‚Äù (√°matim bƒÅÃÅdhamƒÅnƒÅ). Again this phrase supports the notion that the referent of sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- is a cow. In I.53.4 and X.42.10 √°mati- is overcome by cows; the word is paired with hunger (k·π£√∫dh-) in VIII.66.14, X.42.10, and X.43.3. Hunger and neglect can be combatted with cows and their nourishing products, and one of the combatants is Sasarparƒ´.

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§∏‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•Ä‡§É) ‡§≠‡•É‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§£‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§Æ‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¨‡§æ‡§ß‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ) ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä (‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§§‡•ç) (‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‡•á (‡§ú‡§Æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ) ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡•§ ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•à ‡§ú‡§Æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ã‡§∑‡§ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§§‡§™‡§•‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡•á‡•§ (‡§Ü) (‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) (‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§∑‡§æ (‡§§‡§§‡§æ‡§®) ‡§§‡§®‡•Å‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•É‡§£‡•ã‡§§‡§ø (‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§É) ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å (‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§ú‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•à‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•ç‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á. ‡§ú‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§∏‡•á ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç‡§® ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ï‡§°‡•Ç‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§§ ‡§§‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡§æ ‡§®‡•á‡§π‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§≥‡§§‡•á. ‡•• ‡•ß‡•´ ‡••

Although I do not think all the puzzles are ultimately solvable, some clues can get us some distance. First, sasarparƒ´ÃÅ- is a v·πõkƒ´-type fem., and as Debrunner points out (AiG II.2.369), the major use of this inflectional type is for female beings (human and animal). That vs. 14 concerns the ritually worthless cows of the Kƒ´ka·π≠as would also support a contrast with an eminently worthy cow found among us. ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§∏‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•Ä‡§É) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§≠‡§∞‡§§‡•ç) (‡§§‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§ò‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§è‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É (‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø) ‡§â‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡•á (‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å) ‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§∏‡•Å ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å (‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å (‡§∏‡§æ) (‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä (‡§®‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§®‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ (‡§Ü‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§®‡§æ‡•¶‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§Ç‡•¶‡•®‡•§ ‡•≠‡•§ (‡§¶‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§æ) (‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Æ‡§Æ (‡§™‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Ø‡§É ‡§™‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§µ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ú‡§Æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•à‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á (‡§¶‡§¶‡•Å‡§É) ‡§¶‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡••‡•ß‡•¨‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡•ã‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡•Ä ‡§®‡§µ‡•Ä‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§¶‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡•É‡§§ ‡••‡•ß‡•¨‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡§ø ‚Äò‡§ö‡§ø‡§£‡•ç ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡•ã‡§É’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ö‡§ø‡§£‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ö‡§ø‡§£‡•ã ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§æ‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡•á ‡§â‡§û‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∞‡§•‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§™‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•á‡§Ω‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§ò‡§û‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡§æ‡§†‡§æ‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§∂‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø ‡•§ ‚Äò ‡§∂‡•Ñ ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§Æ‡•á ‡•§ ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∑‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§ï‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMay the horse be steady, the axle be strong, the pole be not defective, the yoke not be rotten; may Indra preserve the two yoke-pins from decay; car with uninjured felloes, be ready for us.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The horses: gƒÅvau gaccheta iti gƒÅvau a≈õvau: gƒÅvƒÅ implies those who go, or, in this plural ce, horses; car be ready for us: Vi≈õvƒÅmitra being about to depart from the sacrifice of SudƒÅs, invokes good fortune for his conveyance

Jamison Brereton

Let the two oxen be steadfast, the axle firm; let the chariot-pole not be torn off, nor the yoke be broken off.
Let Indra keep the two pƒÅtalyas [=pieces of chariot?] from breaking. You with indestructible wheel-rims, escort us. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, this verse and the rest of this little section are reminiscent of the final vs. of III.33.13, against disaster on a journey, specifically there a river crossing.

The hapax pƒÅtaly√® is entirely unclear, besides being a dual referring to some part of the chariot.

On the thematic medial stem d√°da- in the sense ‚Äòhold, keep safe‚Äô, see Got≈ç (1st Class, 171-72, flg. Wackernagel).

Griffith

Strong be the pair of oxen, firm the axles, let not the pole slip nor the yoke be broken.
May Indra, keep the yoke-pins from decaying: attend us, thou whose fellies are uninjured.

Geldner

Die beiden Rinder sollen ausdauernd sein und fest die Achse. Nicht soll die Deichsel abrei√üen, nicht das Joch zerbrechen. Indra soll die beiden Wagenst√ºtzen vor Bruch bewahren. Du, dessen Radfelge nicht Schaden nimmt, steh uns zur Seite!

Grassmann

Stark seien die Riemen, fest die Wagenachse; nicht zersplittere die Deichsel, nicht zerbreche das Joch; Indra bewahre die beiden Radb√ºchsen vor Zertr√ºmmerung, du, dessen Radkr√§nze unversehrt sind, sei uns huldreich.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ! ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§É) ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á (‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§É) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§π‡•Å‡§è (‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á) ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§¶‡§¶‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§ú‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≥‡•Å‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§É) ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§õ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§à‡§∑‡§æ) ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü (‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞‡•å) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§≤ (‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•å) ‡§¨‡•à‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ (‡§Æ‡§æ) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§ø, ‡§∂‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Æ‡§æ) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§ø, ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø) ‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•ã ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§≤ ‡§¨‡•à‡§≤ (‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø, ‡§∏‡§ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã ‡••‡•ß‡•≠‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§¨‡§°‡§º‡•á ‡§â‡§™‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ó‡•å ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•Å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ï‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§Ø ‡§® ‡§¨‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§æ‡§• ‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•á‡§≤ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•≠‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§Æ‡•á ‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§¶‡§¶‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≥‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§à‡§∑‡§æ ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞‡•å ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•å ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø ‡§∂‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞‡•å ‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•å ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ω‡§≠‡§ø ‡§∏‡§ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ ‡••‡•ß‡•≠‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞‡•å) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§≤‡•å (‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡•å) ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§≠‡•å (‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≥‡•Å‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§É (‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§É) ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§õ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Æ‡§æ) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ß‡•á (‡§à‡§∑‡§æ) ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ï‡§É (‡§µ‡§ø) (‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç (‡§Æ‡§æ) (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§µ‡§ø) (‡§∂‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§É) ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á) ‡§™‡§§‡§®‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤‡•á (‡§¶‡§¶‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§É) ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡•á‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§®‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å (‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø) (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§∏‡§ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ) ‡••‡•ß‡•≠‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§π‡•ã‡§™‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§™‡§∂‡§µ‡§É ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§® ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§É ‡••‡•ß‡•≠‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúGive strength, Indra, to our bodies; give strength to our vehicles; (give) strength to our sons and grandsons; that they may live (long); for you are giver of strength.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Put power in our bodies, power in our draft-oxen, Indra‚Äî
power for our kith and kin to live, for you are the giver of power.

Griffith

O Indra, give our bodies strength, strength to the bulls who draw the wains,
Strength to our seed and progeny that they may live, for thou art he who giveth strength.

Geldner

St√§rke verleih unseren Leibern, St√§rke unseren Zugstieren, Indra, St√§rke dem leiblichen Samen zum Leben, denn du bist der St√§rkeverleiher!

Grassmann

Gib, Indra, unsern Leibern Kraft, Kraft unsern Wagenstieren, Kraft zum Leben unsern Kindern und Enkeln, denn du bist es, welcher Kraft gibt.

Elizarenkova

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§¨‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ß‡•á‡§π‡§ø) (‡§§‡§®‡•Ç‡§∑‡•Å) ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•Å (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§¨‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶ (‡§Ö‡§®‡§≥‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å) ‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§¨‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§§‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§¨‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§§‡§®‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ï‡•å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡•å‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ω‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§∏‡•á) ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§π‡§ø) ‡§Ø‡§§‡§É (‡§¨‡§≤‡§¶‡§æ‡§É) (‡§Ö‡§∏‡§ø) ‡••‡•ß‡•Æ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúFix firmly the substance of the khayar (axle), give solidity to the ≈õi≈õu (floor) of the car; strong axle, strongly fixed by us, be strong; cast us not from out of our conveyance.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Khayar and ≈õi≈õu: khadirasya sƒÅram is the text;

Khadira = mimosa catechu of which the bolt of the axle is made; while the ≈õi·πÉ≈õapa, dalbergia sisu furnishes wood for the floor; these are still timber-trees in common use

Jamison Brereton

Engird yourself in the hardwood of the acacia tree; place strength in the ≈õi·πÉ≈õapƒÅ(-wood) in its recoil.
O Axle, you who are firm and were made firm, stay firm. Don‚Äôt make us leave off from this journey. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

On abh√≠ vyayasva see comm. ad VII.33.4. I assume ‚Äòengird‚Äô refers to the ends of the axle being embedded in the wheel hub.

The loc. spandan√© is generally taken as a third type of wood (besides khadir√°and ≈õi·πÉ≈õ√°pƒÅ-), but while the other two words are standard designations of trees, spandan√°- is ordinarily not, but rather refers to a type of motion – jerking or kicking.

I take it to refer here to the elasticity or flexibility of Dalbergia sissoo (≈õi·πÉsapƒÅ-), a quality it has (at least acdg. to the internet).

Griffith

Enclose thee in the heart of Khayar timber, in the car wrought of Sinsapa put firmness.
Show thyself strong, O Axle, fixed and strengthened: throw us not from the car whereon we travel.

(‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≥‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§£‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§∏ (‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á (‡§Æ‡§æ) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§µ, ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§π‡§ø‡§™‡§É) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡••‡•ß‡•Ø‡••

  • ‚Äútowards; ƒÅkƒÅra; until; ƒÅ; since; according to; ƒÅ [suffix].‚Äù
  • vimocanƒÅt ‚Üê vimocana
  • [noun], ablative, singular, neuter

‡§Ø‡§ú‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Ø ‡§Ü‡§∂‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§•‡•ã ‡§∞‡§•‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§¢‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§π‡§æ‡§É ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§§‡•Å ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö ‡§Ü ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•Å ‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ü ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•à ‡•§ ‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§µ‡•á‡§ó‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ü ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•Å ‡•• ‡§π‡§æ‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ì‡§π‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡•á’ ‡•§ ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡§ø ‚Äò‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§ò‡§∏’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§∏‡§ø‡§ö‡•ã ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡•§ ‚Äò‡§ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§æ‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑ ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡§ø ‡§ö‡§ô‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§ò‡§æ‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•á‡§É ‚Äò‡§ó‡•á‡§π‡•á ‡§ï‡§É’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§É ‡•§ ‚Äò‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•à ‡•§ ’ ‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø’ ‡•§ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§ï‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ã ‡§≤‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡§É ‡•§ ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§É ‡•§ ‡§è‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§É ‡•• ‡•• ‡•®‡•® ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMay this lord of the forest never desert us nor do us harm; may we travel prosperously home until the stopping (of the car), until the unharnessing (of the steeds).‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

This lord of the forest: vanaspati, i.e. the timber of which the car is made

Jamison Brereton

Let this Lord of the Forest [=tree] here not leave us behind and not cause us harm.
(Let there be) well-being all the way to the houses, to the unharnessing, to the unhitching. ·≥ì

Griffith

Let not this sovran of the wood leave us forlorn or injure us.
Safe may we be until we reach our homes and rest us and unyoke.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç ! ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á (‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§π (‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§® ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ (‡§Æ‡§æ) ‡§Æ‡§§ (‡§π‡§æ‡§É) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§ö) ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§Æ‡§æ, ‡§ö) ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç (‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ú‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡•§ ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ü, ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•à) ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§Ü, ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§π‡•ã (‡§Ü, ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§§‡§ï ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á ‡••‡•®‡•¶‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡•á ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•à‡§∏‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§∏‡§¨‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§®‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•®‡•¶‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç ! ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§π‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡•à‡§µ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§µ‡§∏‡•à ‡§Ü ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡•Å ‡••‡•®‡•¶‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) (‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï‡§É (‡§Æ‡§æ) (‡§ö) (‡§π‡§æ‡§É) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•á‡§É (‡§Æ‡§æ) (‡§ö) (‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç) ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ü) (‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) (‡§Ü) (‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡•à) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∑‡•ã ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ã‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§≤‡•ã‡§™‡§æ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ (‡§Ü) (‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡••‡•®‡•¶‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§ï‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡••‡•®‡•¶‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á. ‡§ú‡§∂‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§±‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡§§‡§æ‡§§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§®‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§µ‡•á ‡§µ ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç ‡§®‡§Ø‡•á. ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç ‡§®‡§Ø‡•á. ‡•• ‡•®‡•¶ ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúIndra, hero,possessor of wealth, protect us this day against our foes with many and excellent defences; may the vile wretch who hates us fall (before us); may the breath of life depart from him whom we hate.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Indra, with your manifold means of help, which are the best possible ones, quicken us today, bounteous champion.
Whoever hates us, let him fall low. Whomever we hate, let breath
leave him. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are traditionally taken as depicting the rivalry between Vi≈õvƒÅmitra and Vasi·π£·π≠ha, but I see no sign of this here; certainly Vasi·π£·π≠ha is not mentioned. The verbal link is supposed to be VII.104.16 adham√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a ‚Äúlet him fall lowest,‚Äù a curse uttered in a Vasi·π£·π≠ha hymn that echoes our 21c √°dhara·∏• s√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a. But in neither case is the opponent named, and there is no reason to assume that Vasi·π£·π≠ha directs this at Vi≈õvƒÅmitra or vice versa.

The first vs. of this sequence (21) is quite straightforward; vs. 22 is more complex, but I feel fairly confident in its interpr. But vss. 23-24 are very difficult, and my interpr. is correspondingly quite provisional.

With various aids this day come to us, Indra, with best aids speed us, Maghavan, thou Hero.
Let him who hatcth us fall headlong downward: him whom we hate let vital breath abandon.

(‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§É) ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ö (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§µ‡•à‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à (‡§∏‡§É) ‡§µ‡§π ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§™‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á (‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•ã (‡§â) ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó (‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§É) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç (‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ (‡§â) ‡§≠‡•Ä (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§É) ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å (‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•Å) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡•§ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§π‡•á (‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§ß‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§∂‡•Ç‡§∞) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§™ (‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§§ (‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ (‡§ä‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§®‡§É) ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á (‡§Ö‡§™, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§ú‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡••‡•®‡•ß‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§® ‡§Ö‡§™‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç, ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ï‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§® ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç ‡••‡•®‡•ß‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ! ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ß‡§∞‡•ã ‡§®‡•ã ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§∏ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡•ã ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ú‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ ‡••‡•®‡•ß‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§ä‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É (‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø) (‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§ß‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É (‡§Æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§™‡•Ç‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§ß‡§®‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§∂‡•Ç‡§∞) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ï (‡§ú‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø (‡§Ø‡§É) (‡§®‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç (‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø) ‡§µ‡•à‡§∞‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§É) ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ö‡§É (‡§∏‡§É) (‡§™‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§â) (‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ) (‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§â) (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§É) (‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•Å) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§§‡•Å ‡••‡•®‡•ß‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•à‡§µ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡•á ‡§ö ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§®‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§§‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É ‡••‡•®‡•ß‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• -‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§® ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§±‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑ ‡§µ ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•á‡§ö‡•á ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£ ‡§µ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§†‡•Ä ‡§ú‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á‡§§ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§®‡§æ ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç‡§® ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§® ‡§µ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ó‡•à‡§∞‡•á‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡•á. ‡•• ‡•®‡•ß ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúAs (the tree) suffers pain from the axe, as the ≈õimbal flower is (easily) cut off, as the injured cauldron leaking scatters foam, so may mine enemy perish.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The ellipse: as the tree is cut down by the axe, so may the enemy be cut down; as one cuts off without difficulty the flower of the ≈õimbal, so may he be destroyed; as the ukhƒÅ (cauldron) when struck (prahata), and thence leaking (ye·π£antƒ´, sravantƒ´), scatters foam or breath from its mouth, so (dve·π£·π≠a madƒ´ya, mantra sƒÅmarthyena prahata·∏• san, phenam mukhƒÅd udgirtu), may that hater, struck by the power of my prayer vomit foam fromhis mouth

Jamison Brereton

(Though) he heats his axe all the way through, it‚Äôs just a ≈õimbala flower he hacks off.
The ukhƒÅ-pot, (though) boiling, boiling over, just throws off foam,
o Indra. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

With Oldenberg, I reject the interpr. of cid in abc as a simile particle (contra SƒÅya·πáa and Geldner); in all three cases the cid can be interpreted in its usual ‚Äòeven, even though, just‚Äô sense. However, I differ from Oldenberg on the purport of the vs. He thinks it describes concrete events, possibly as a ‚ÄúBeschreibung von Zauberhandlungen,‚Äù while I think it contains two figurative descriptions of the impotence of the enemy – in this I am closer to Geldner‚Äôs notion of similes than to Oldenberg I also find myself in the odd position of being in general agreement with Griffith‚Äôs interpr. (based on Ludwig‚Äôs).

Each hemistich describes an action involving great effort and drama that produces trivial and insubstantial results. In ab an ax is thoroughly heated, but this formidable weapon only cuts off the blossom of a silk cotton tree. (That silk cotton tree flowers are a vivid red might remind the audience of the real blood that might have been shed by a blazing hot ax.) In cd a pot, also heated, is boiling (y√©·π£antƒ´), indeed has boiled over (pr√°yastƒÅ), but all it produces is foam.

21-24 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are traditionally taken as depicting the rivalry between Vi≈õvƒÅmitra and Vasi·π£·π≠ha, but I see no sign of this here; certainly Vasi·π£·π≠ha is not mentioned. The verbal link is supposed to be VII.104.16 adham√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a ‚Äúlet him fall lowest,‚Äù a curse uttered in a Vasi·π£·π≠ha hymn that echoes our 21c √°dhara·∏• s√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a. But in neither case is the opponent named, and there is no reason to assume that Vasi·π£·π≠ha directs this at Vi≈õvƒÅmitra or vice versa.

The first vs. of this sequence (21) is quite straightforward; vs. 22 is more complex, but I feel fairly confident in its interpr. But vss. 23-24 are very difficult, and my interpr. is correspondingly quite provisional.

Griffith

He heats his very axe, and then cuts a mere Semal blossom off.
O Indra, like a caldron cracked and seething, so he pours out foam.

Geldner

Er macht ihn hei√ü wie man eine Axt erhitzt, er zerhackt ihn wie den Simbalabaum. Wie ein siedender Topf, der √ºberkocht, wirft er Schaum aus, o Indra.

Grassmann

Ein jedes Blatt [palƒÅ√ß√°m vermuthe ich statt para√ß√∫m] brennt er hinweg, und jede Bl√ºte bricht ab; jeder Topf, o Indra, wenn er kocht und √ºbersiedet, wirft er Schaum.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§™‡§∞‡§∂‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§†‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§µ‡§ø) (‡§§‡§™‡§§‡§ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§∂‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∂‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡§≤‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§µ‡§ø) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡•á‡§£ (‡§µ‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§§‡§ø) ‡§õ‡§ø‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§â‡§ñ‡§æ) ‡§™‡§æ‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä (‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ (‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ (‡§´‡•á‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•®‡•®‡••

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•Ä‡§∞‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§ ‡§è‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡••‡•®‡•®‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMen, (the might) of the destroyer is not known toyou; regarding him as a mere animal, they lead him away desirous (silently to complete his devotions); the wise condescend not to turn the foolish into ridicule, they do not lead the ass before the horse.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Legend: Vi≈õvƒÅmitra was seized and bound by the followers of Vasi·π£·π≠ha, when observing a vow of silence. These were the reflections of the sage on the occasion: disparaging the rivalry of Vasi·π£·π≠ha with himself, as if between an ass and a horse: sƒÅyakasya = of an arrow;here explained, to destroy, avasƒÅnakƒÅri·πáa·∏•; lodham nayanti = they lead the sage;

Lodha = from lubdham, desirous that his penance might not be frustrated, tapasa·∏• k·π£ayo mƒÅ bh≈´d iti, lobhena tu·π£·πáƒ´m sthitam ·πõ·π£im pa≈õum manyamƒÅna, thinking the ·πõ·π£i silent through his desire, to be an animal, i.e., stupid; another interpretation in Nirukta:lubdham ·πõ·π£im nayanti pa≈õum manyamƒÅna·∏•, they take away the desiring ·πõ·π£i, thinking him an animal; na avƒÅjinam vƒÅjinƒÅ hƒÅsayanti: vƒÅjina = from vac, speed, with ina affix; interpreted as srvaj√±a, all knowing; the contrary avajina = m≈´rkha, a fool

There is no notice taken of his missile, you people. They lead a lump, thinking it a beast.
(But) they are not making a non-prizewinner race with a prizewinner, nor leading a donkey before the horses. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

In my opinion, at least the first half of this vs. continues the sentiment of vs. 22: the enemy is powerless, despite bluff and bluster. In pƒÅda a older translations supply an unidentified subject (‚Äúhe‚Äù) for cikite, but the medial perfect of ‚àöcit is always pass.-intrans. and with K√ºmmel (176) I take the verb as an impersonal passive with an oblique subj. in the genitive; cf. I.51.7 t√°va v√°jra≈õ cikite with the subject in the more normal nom. The point here is that the opponent‚Äôs missile (sƒÅÃÅyaka-) is so inconsequential as not to attract or deserve notice.

In b the first problem is the hapax lodh√°-, which is universally taken as a red animal of some sort, a horse (Geldner, Renou), fox (Grassmann), or goat (Oldenberg, tentatively EWA). My quite different tr. ‚Äòclod‚Äô assumes (again, very tentatively) an association with a loose set of words for lump, clod, etc.: lo·π≠·π≠ha (Pkt., etc. =Vedic lo·π£·π≠√°-, perhaps by hypersanskritization) (Turner 11157), *lottha / *lodda / *loddha (Tu. 11137), *lu·π≠·π≠a (Tu 11077). The point would then be that ‚Äúthey‚Äù (whoever they are) lead (to sacrifice?) a lump of earth or the like, thinking that it‚Äôs an actual (sacrificial?) beast.

This situation reminds us of the chariot race of Mudgala and MudgalƒÅnƒ´ (X.102) in which a block of wood is yoked with a bull, and the oddly assorted pair still wins the race (X.102.8-9). It might also remind us of X.28.9 in which an earth clod (log√°-, not too distant phonologically) splits a stone. The other morphological fact of note in this pƒÅda is that p√°≈õu is neut. and initially accented (versus the ubiquitous masc. pa≈õ√∫-).

This may be a deep archaism, matching Lat. pecu, Goth. faihu (cf. AiG II.1.20, II.2.474), or it may be a nonce attempt to de-animatize the word in this peculiar context.

Like vs. 22 and 23a, 23b describes an undesirable situation exactly contrary to what was aimed at: having mistaken a lump for a sacrificial animal, the actors will surely not get the results they wanted – although X.102.8-9 and X.28.9 may point to success in unlikely circumstances. But the second hemistich depicts situations in which, though a mistake was possible, it was not made: they don‚Äôt set a bad horse (√°vƒÅjin-) to race with a good one; they don‚Äôt put a donkey before the horses. The question is whether the subjects of the three 3rd pl. verbs (nayanti b, hƒÅsayanti c, nayanti again d) are the same, or are the deluded weaklings of ab being contrasted with more clear-headed and successful actors in cd? Common sense suggests the latter, but the morphologically identical sequence of verbs with no overt subject or change of subject the former (as Oldenberg points out). I cannot make up my mind, esp.

because the following vs. muddies the waters even further.

Given the interpretational difficulties, the grammatical identity of √°≈õvƒÅn is a minor problem. In the published translation I take it as acc. pl. √°≈õvƒÅn, but the Pp. interprets it as abl. √°≈õvƒÅt, with the final -n in sandhi before the nasal of nayanti. Either is possible (‚Äúlead before the horses‚Äù / ‚Äúlead before the horse‚Äù), and given the uncertainty of meaning, there is no obvious way to choose. The abl. is supposed to be construed with pur√°·∏•, but in fact ABL. + pur√°·∏• is not a robust construction.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are traditionally taken as depicting the rivalry between Vi≈õvƒÅmitra and Vasi·π£·π≠ha, but I see no sign of this here; certainly Vasi·π£·π≠ha is not mentioned. The verbal link is supposed to be VII.104.16 adham√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a ‚Äúlet him fall lowest,‚Äù a curse uttered in a Vasi·π£·π≠ha hymn that echoes our 21c √°dhara·∏• s√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a. But in neither case is the opponent named, and there is no reason to assume that Vasi·π£·π≠ha directs this at Vi≈õvƒÅmitra or vice versa.

The first vs. of this sequence (21) is quite straightforward; vs. 22 is more complex, but I feel fairly confident in its interpr. But vss. 23-24 are very difficult, and my interpr. is correspondingly quite provisional.

Are these the sons of Bharata, Indra? They take note of the non-meal, not the meal.
They spur the alien horse, not their own. At the contest they lead
around the one whose prize is (just) a bowstring. ·≥ì

Jamison Brereton Notes

The good sense / bad sense problem continues, or returns, here. The sons of Bharata are ceremonially presented to us, with the here-and-now deictic im√©. And we know from vs. 12 that the Bharata people (bhƒÅÃÅrata- j√°na-) are our people. But here they (or the subjects of the three 3rd pl. verbs: cikitu·∏• b, hinv√°nti c, p√°ri ·πáayanti d) seem to make the same bad choices that were visible in vss. 22-23, esp. 23b. The continuation of the 3rd pl. verbs invites us to make the Bharatas subjects also in vs. 22.

Is this a jokey anti-dƒÅnastuti? Are the ‚Äúbad choices‚Äù I just mentioned meant to show that even if they do stupid things, they will still beat the incompetents depicted in vs. 22? Or that bad choices can still sometimes unaccountably lead to good? I am baffled.

The mealtime prapitv√°- is well attested in the RV, but apapitv√°- is found only here. Both Geldner and Renou take the words in some kind of figurative sense (e.g., Renou apapitv√°- ‚Äòla retraite‚Äô, prapitv√°- ‚Äòl‚Äô√©lan-en-avant‚Äô), but since the -pitv√°- compounds are otherwise only used of meals and the times of day associated with them, it seems best to maintain that sense here; so Witzel Got≈ç ‚Äòdie Nachessenzeit‚Äô, ‚Äòdie Voressenzeit‚Äô. In its contrast with pra here, I take apa as meaning ‚Äòleaving the meal, post-prandial‚Äô and therefore ‚Äònon-meal‚Äô. My interpr. is influenced by my sense that the lesser choice is the one being made in each case in this vs. In c the standard tr. take n√° as a simile marker: ‚Äúthey incite their own horse like an alien one.‚Äù But given the paired negative clauses in 23cd and the undoubted negative (or at least undoubted by the standard tr.) in the immediately preceding pƒÅda (24b), where the n√° takes the same position as in c, the pattern seems to impose another negative here. Under either interpr. the action is not a very smart one: spurring your own horse like an alien one should presumably mean that you don‚Äôt spur it at all.

The accentuation of jyƒÅÃÄ- in the bahuvrƒ´hi jyƒÅÃÄ-vƒÅja-, against simplex jyƒÅÃÅ-, is attributed to the shift to initial accent in some other bahuvrƒ´his: AiG II.1.293 with Nachtr. 81. The standard tr. avoid the problem of the sense of this cmpd by attributing to -vƒÅja- a sense it doesn‚Äôt otherwise have: Geldner (Witzel Got≈ç; cf. Grassmann) Schnelligkeit, Renou la force. But vƒÅÃÅja- means ‚Äòprize‚Äô and bahuvrƒ´his with it as 2nd member ‚Äòhaving X as prize‚Äô. I here assume that winning only a bowstring (minus the bow) would not be a glorious outcome.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, these vss. are traditionally taken as depicting the rivalry between Vi≈õvƒÅmitra and Vasi·π£·π≠ha, but I see no sign of this here; certainly Vasi·π£·π≠ha is not mentioned. The verbal link is supposed to be VII.104.16 adham√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a ‚Äúlet him fall lowest,‚Äù a curse uttered in a Vasi·π£·π≠ha hymn that echoes our 21c √°dhara·∏• s√°s padƒ´·π£·π≠a. But in neither case is the opponent named, and there is no reason to assume that Vasi·π£·π≠ha directs this at Vi≈õvƒÅmitra or vice versa.

The first vs. of this sequence (21) is quite straightforward; vs. 22 is more complex, but I feel fairly confident in its interpr. But vss. 23-24 are very difficult, and my interpr. is correspondingly quite provisional.

These men, the sons of Bharata, O Indra, regard not severance or close connexion.
They urge their own steed as it were another’s, and take him, swift as the bow’s string, to battle.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§π‡•á (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ! ‡§Ü‡§™‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á (‡§≠‡§∞‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§É) ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§á‡§Æ‡•á) ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Æ‡•á‡§∞‡•á (‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§¶‡•É‡§∂ ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§ï ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ó (‡§Ö‡§™‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ò‡•ã‡§°‡§º‡•á ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§£‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§ï‡•á (‡§®) ‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§¨‡§¢‡§º‡§æ‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ü‡§ú‡•å) ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ß‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§§‡§æ‡§Å‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§™‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ (‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§¶‡•É‡§∂ ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã ‡••‡•®‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§¶‡•É‡§∂ ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•ã‡§µ‡•á ‡••‡•®‡•™‡••‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§ø‡§ú‡•Å‡§≤‡•Ä, ‡§Æ‡•á‡§ò, ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§™‡§ø‡§õ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§æ‡§• ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡•• ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§™‡§®‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§§‡•á‡§à‡§∏‡§µ‡§æ‡§Å ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∏‡§∞‡•á ‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§≤ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ö‡•å‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§É

‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø: ‡§π‡•á ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ! ‡§§‡§µ ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§≠‡§∞‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§® ‡§Ø ‡§á‡§Æ‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§á‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ω‡§™‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ú‡•å ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§Å‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡••‡•®‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É

‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§á‡§Æ‡•á) (‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§ï (‡§≠‡§∞‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∂‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§á‡§µ ‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É (‡§Ö‡§™‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ö‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§É (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§®) ‡§á‡§µ (‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§™‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É (‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) (‡§Ü‡§ú‡•å) ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•á ‡••‡•®‡•™‡••

‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§π‡§ø) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•®‡•™‡•• ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á‡§ò‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡•• ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§≤‡•á ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡••

‡§∏‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã‡§∂‡•Ä ‚Üê ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶-‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä (‡§Æ) - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§ ‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§π‡•á. ‡§ú‡•á ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•á ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§™‡§≤‡§æ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§µ ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§æ‡§£‡§§‡§æ‡§§, (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä‡§≤ ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞) ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•Ä‡§≤ ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•á ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§§ ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§¶‡•à‡§µ ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡•á.
